5c:["$","p",null,{"style":{"fontSize":"1rem","color":"var(--text-body)","lineHeight":1.75,"marginBottom":"1.25em","marginTop":0},"children":[["$","strong",null,{"style":{"color":"var(--text-strong)","fontWeight":600},"children":"Joint space actions"}]," (torques or positions) provide maximum expressive control but\nrequire the policy to learn the full inverse kinematics and dynamics of the robot. 5d:["$","p",null,{"style":{"fontSize":"1rem","color":"var(--text-body)","lineHeight":1.75,"marginBottom":"1.25em","marginTop":0},"children":[["$","strong",null,{"style":{"color":"var(--text-strong)","fontWeight":600},"children":"End-effector Cartesian commands"}]," specify the desired position, orientation, or velocity of\nthe end-effector, with an inverse kinematics solver converting these to joint commands. 6b:["$","p",null,{"style":{"fontSize":"1rem","color":"var(--text-body)","lineHeight":1.75,"marginBottom":"1.25em","marginTop":0},"children":"Learning a complex manipulation task from a random policy initialization is typically\ninfeasible: the probability of reaching the goal from random actions is near zero, the\nresulting zero-reward trajectories provide no useful gradient signal, and the policy cannot\nimprove. 6c:["$","p",null,{"style":{"fontSize":"1rem","color":"var(--text-body)","lineHeight":1.75,"marginBottom":"1.25em","marginTop":0},"children":["A curriculum can vary difficulty along multiple axes: ",["$","strong",null,{"style":{"color":"var(--text-strong)","fontWeight":600},"children":"initial state distributions"}]," (start\nwith the object already in the robot's hand before training pick-and-place), ",["$","strong",null,{"style":{"color":"var(--text-strong)","fontWeight":600},"children":"tolerances"}],"\n(require coarser position accuracy early in training, then tighten), ",["$","strong",null,{"style":{"color":"var(--text-strong)","fontWeight":600},"children":"horizon lengths"}],"\n(train on short-horizon tasks that require fewer sequential decisions), or ",["$","strong",null,{"style":{"color":"var(--text-strong)","fontWeight":600},"children":"physics\ncomplexity"}]," (simple frictionless grasping before contact-rich assembly)."]}]
6d:["$","p",null,{"style":{"fontSize":"1rem","color":"var(--text-body)","lineHeight":1.75,"marginBottom":"1.25em","marginTop":0},"children":"Automatic curriculum generation methods — including POET (Paired Open-Ended Trailblazers)\nand the universal value function approach — adaptively adjust task difficulty based on\nthe agent's current performance, keeping the task in the \"zone of proximal development\"\nwhere it is challenging but solvable. 74:["$","h2",null,{"id":"key-takeaways","className":"heading-linked","style":{"fontFamily":"var(--font-display)","fontWeight":600,"fontSize":"1.375rem","color":"var(--text-strong)","letterSpacing":"-0.01em","lineHeight":1.25,"marginBottom":"0.6em","marginTop":"2.5em","paddingBottom":"0.4em","borderBottom":"1px solid var(--border-subtle)","scrollMarginTop":"90px"},"children":["Key takeaways",["$","$L30",null,{"id":"key-takeaways"}]]}]
75:["$","p",null,{"style":{"fontSize":"1rem","color":"var(--text-body)","lineHeight":1.75,"marginBottom":"1.25em","marginTop":0},"children":["Robotic ",["$","$L2f",null,{"term":"RL"}]," operates under hard constraints — sample efficiency, physical safety, and\ndifficult resets — that dominate algorithm selection. Off-policy methods, especially ",["$","$L2f",null,{"term":"SAC"}],"\nand TD3, are standard because they reuse all collected experience through replay buffers.\n",["$","$L2f",null,{"term":"SAC"}],"'s maximum entropy objective provides automatic exploration through policy entropy\nregularization, removing the need for separate noise injection. Safety is enforced at\nmultiple levels: hardware workspace limits, CBF-QP filters at the policy output, and\nshielded controllers for emergency recovery. Action space design has large effects on\nlearning efficiency; end-effector Cartesian delta commands are the most common choice for\nmanipulation. Potential-based reward shaping is theoretically principled; non-potential\nshaping introduces unintended optimal policies. Curriculum learning is essential for\ncomplex tasks where random initialization cannot reach the goal. The ",["$","$L2f",null,{"term":"RL"}]," choices made for\nrobotics and for language models differ systematically because the cost of data generation\ndiffers by orders of magnitude between domains."]}]

7a:["$","$L2e",null,{"type":"tip","title":"Solutions","children":["$","ol",null,{"style":{"paddingLeft":"1.5em","marginBottom":"1.25em","color":"var(--text-body)","lineHeight":1.75},"children":["\n",["$","li",null,{"style":{"marginBottom":"0.35em","fontSize":"1rem"},"children":[["$","strong",null,{"style":{"color":"var(--text-strong)","fontWeight":600},"children":"SAC sparse reward on hardware."}]," 10,000 interactions is far too few for sparse-reward RL — random exploration essentially never triggers success, so there is no gradient, and hardware is sample-limited. Redesign: a dense shaped reward (distance-to-hole plus alignment), a curriculum (start with the peg near/above the hole and widen), demonstrations seeding the replay buffer (or offline pretraining / residual RL on a scripted controller), and goal relabeling (HER). Each supplies learning signal or shrinks the exploration burden."]}],"\n",["$","li",null,{"style":{"marginBottom":"0.35em","fontSize":"1rem"},"children":[["$","strong",null,{"style":{"color":"var(--text-strong)","fontWeight":600},"children":"Persistent small CBF correction."}]," Because the executed (clipped) action differs from the proposed one, you must train on the ",["$","strong",null,{"style":{"color":"var(--text-strong)","fontWeight":600},"children":"executed"}]," actions to converge to the CBF-constrained optimum — training on proposed actions never lets the policy experience the true consequences, so it won't converge. Interference appears when corrections are large or frequent and credit is assigned to the wrong action; detect it by monitoring proposed-vs-executed action divergence and reward plateaus while corrections persist."]}],"\n",["$","li",null,{"style":{"marginBottom":"0.35em","fontSize":"1rem"},"children":[["$","strong",null,{"style":{"color":"var(--text-strong)","fontWeight":600},"children":"Action space for bimanual."}]," Cartesian end-effector deltas make inter-arm spatial coordination easier (the task lives in relative-pose space) but hide dynamics; joint-torque control gives full authority including forces but forces the policy to learn coordination and IK implicitly. A good analytical IK makes Cartesian even more attractive. If contact forces must be controlled explicitly, the answer flips toward torque/impedance control, since position deltas cannot regulate force."]}],"\n",["$","li",null,{"style":{"marginBottom":"0.35em","fontSize":"1rem"},"children":[["$","strong",null,{"style":{"color":"var(--text-strong)","fontWeight":600},"children":"Reward hacking."}]," The violated principle is that shaping should be ",["$","strong",null,{"style":{"color":"var(--text-strong)","fontWeight":600},"children":"potential-based"}]," to preserve the optimal policy — but Ng et al. only guarantees policy invariance under correct dynamics; it cannot prevent exploiting a simulator artifact, because the jerky motion is genuinely optimal in the wrong sim dynamics. Redesign: make the proximity term potential-based and physically grounded, penalize jerk/torque, and add domain randomization so the exploit is not robust across dynamics."]}],"\n",["$","li",null,{"style":{"marginBottom":"0.35em","fontSize":"1rem"},"children":[["$","strong",null,{"style":{"color":"var(--text-strong)","fontWeight":600},"children":["SAC temperature ",["$","span",null,{"className":"katex","children":[["$","span",null,{"className":"katex-mathml","children":["$","math",null,{"xmlns":"http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML","children":["$","semantics",null,{"children":[["$","mrow",null,{"children":["$","mi",null,{"children":"α"}]}],["$","annotation",null,{"encoding":"application/x-tex","children":"\\alpha"}]]}]}]}],["$","span",null,{"className":"katex-html","aria-hidden":"true","children":["$","span",null,{"className":"base","children":[["$","span",null,{"className":"strut","style":{"height":"0.4306em"}}],["$","span",null,{"className":"mord mathnormal","style":{"marginRight":"0.0037em"},"children":"α"}]]}]}]]}],"."]}]," Large ","$L132"," drives high entropy: lots of exploration but more unsafe actions near joint limits early and slower, less precise convergence; small ","$L133"," is greedy early, under-explores contact, but makes fewer unsafe excursions.
